[4:2:344,469] independent entities under the California  

[5:0:344,479] Constitution, or are departments with a recognized  

[5:1:344,489] need to operate outside of the administrative  

[5:2:344,499] oversight and control of an agency secretary.  

[5:3:344,509] Constitutionally-established bodies include the  

[5:4:344,519] Legislature, Judicial Branch, Governor's Office,  

[5:5:344,529] and constitutional officers. This section also  

[6:0:344,539] includes such independent entities as the Office of  

[6:1:344,549] the Inspector General and the California State  

[6:2:344,559] Lottery Commission.

[1:0:268,39] 0110   Senate  

[2:0:66,61] The Legislature is comprised of two houses, the Senate and the Assembly, and is responsible for enacting laws and  

[2:1:66,71] establishing public policy for California. The State Senate is comprised of 40 members who are elected for 4-year terms.  

[2:2:66,81] Members of the Legislature may serve a maximum of 12 years total in the Senate, the Assembly, or both, in any combination of  

[2:3:66,91] terms. Members first elected before June 6, 2012, however, may generally serve a maximum of 14 years total, with no more  

[2:4:66,101] than two terms in the Senate and three terms in the Assembly.

[6:1:66,229] The Legislature is comprised of two houses, the Senate and the Assembly, and is responsible for enacting laws and  

[6:2:66,239] establishing public policy for California. Both houses contribute to support the Legislative Analyst's Office, which provides fiscal  

[6:3:66,250] and policy advice to the Legislature, and is overseen by the 16-member bipartisan Joint Legislative Budget Committee.  

[6:4:66,267] Because the Legislature's   programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Legislature has a capital outlay program to  

[6:5:66,278] support this need.   For the specifics on the Legislature's   capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[3:0:66,69] The California State Capitol, which serves as the state’s working seat of government, began construction in 1860 and was  

[3:1:66,79] completed in 1874. The Capitol remained largely unchanged until 1949, when additional space requirements drove the need for  

[3:2:66,89] construction of the Capitol Annex, which attaches to the east side of the original Capitol building.   The Annex, which was  

[3:3:66,99] completed in 1951, houses legislative offices, committee rooms, and the Governor’s offices.  

[3:4:66,117] Government Code Section 9112(a) authorizes the Joint Rules Committee to pursue the construction of a new, restored,  

[3:5:66,127] rehabilitated, renovated, or reconstructed capitol building annex and associated projects.

[10:0:139,471] 0150   Contributions to the Legislators' Retirement System  

[10:1:66,492] Established in 1947, the Legislators' Retirement System provides retirement and death benefits for legislators, constitutional  

[10:2:66,502] officers, and legislative statutory officers.  

[11:0:66,523] The California Public Employees' Retirement System (CalPERS) administers the Legislators' Retirement System on behalf of  

[11:1:66,533] its members. Retirement benefits paid are based upon age, years of service, and final compensation. Benefits for death,  

[11:2:66,543] disability, and surviving dependents of deceased members are also provided under specific conditions.   The Legislators'  

[11:3:66,553] Retirement System is funded through contributions from the state, members of the system, and investment earnings. 

[12:0:66,574] Proposition 140, approved by voters in 1990, closed the Legislators' Retirement System to all new legislators after November  

[12:1:66,584] 6, 1990.   Subsequently, the California Public Employees' Pension Reform Act of 2013, Chapter 296, Statutes of 2012 (AB 340)  

[12:2:66,594] closed the Legislators' Retirement System to all new members effective January 1, 2013. New constitutional officers, statutory  

[12:3:66,604] officers, Sergeant-at-Arms, and the Insurance Commissioner retain optional membership rights in CalPERS.

[6:0:208,203] 0160   Legislative Counsel Bureau  

[6:1:66,224] The Legislative Counsel Bureau (Bureau) provides legal assistance to the two houses of the Legislature, and their members  

[6:2:66,234] and committees, by resolving a large volume of complex legal problems arising in connection with the legislative process. The  

[6:3:66,244] legal services furnished include rendering opinions, drafting bills, counseling, attending meetings of legislative committees as  

[6:4:66,255] counsel, and representing the Legislature in litigation. The attorney-client relationship is maintained and all work is confidential.  

[7:0:66,275] In addition, the Bureau prepares and provides necessary indices and appropriate tables necessary to identify legislative  

[7:1:66,285] measures and compiles and indexes statutes and codes.  

[8:0:66,306] The Bureau operates the Legislative Data Center, which provides information technology services in support of the legislative  

[8:1:66,316] information system and the processing of legislative measures.  

[9:0:66,336] Pursuant to Chapter 46 of the Statutes of 2012, the California Commission on Uniform State Laws was transferred into the  

[9:1:66,346] Bureau. The California Commission on Uniform State Laws presents to the Legislature uniform laws recommended by the  

[9:2:66,357] National Conference of Commissioners on Uniform State Laws and then promotes passage of these uniform acts.  

[9:3:66,374] Pursuant to Chapter 450 of the Statutes of 2018, the Bureau provides advice and investigation services to the Legislature  

[9:4:66,384] related to workplace misconduct through the Workplace Conduct Unit.

[11:0:242,576] 0250   Judicial Branch  

[12:0:66,597] Article VI of the Constitution creates the Supreme Court of California and the Courts of Appeal to exercise the judicial power of  

[12:1:66,608] the state at the appellate level. Article VI also creates the Judicial Council of California to administer the state's judicial system.  

[12:2:66,618] Chapter 869, Statutes of 1997, created the California Habeas Corpus Resource Center to represent any person financially  

[12:3:66,628] unable to employ appellate counsel in capital cases.  

[13:0:66,648] The Lockyer-Isenberg Trial Court Funding Act of 1997 (Chapter 850, Statutes of 1997) provided a stable and consistent  

[13:1:66,659] funding source for the trial courts. Beginning with fiscal year 1997-98, consolidation of the costs of trial court operation was  

[13:2:66,669] implemented at the state level, with the exception of facility, revenue collection, and local judicial benefit costs. This  

[13:3:66,679] implementation capped the counties' general purpose revenue contributions to trial court costs at a revised 1994-95 level. The  

[13:4:66,689] county contributions become part of the Trial Court Trust Fund, which supports all trial court operations. Fine and penalty  

[13:5:66,699] revenue collected by each county is retained or distributed in accordance with statute. Each county makes quarterly payments  

[13:6:66,710] to the Trial Court Trust Fund equal to the fine and penalty revenue received by the state General Fund in 1994-95, as adjusted

[3:0:66,69] by amounts equivalent to specified fine and fee revenues that counties benefited from in 2003-04. The Trial Court Facilities Act  

[3:1:66,79] of 2002 (Chapter 1082, statutes of 2002) provided a process for the responsibility for court facilities to be transferred from the  

[3:2:66,89] counties to the state by July 1, 2007, which was extended to December 31, 2009, by Chapter 9, Statutes of 2008. The Trial  

[4:0:66,99] Court Facilities Act of 2002 also established several new revenue sources, effective on January 1, 2003. These revenues are  

[4:1:66,109] deposited into the State Court Facilities Construction Fund for the purpose of funding the construction and maintenance of  

[5:0:66,120] court facilities throughout the state. Counties contribute revenues for the ongoing operation and maintenance of court facilities  

[5:1:66,130] based upon historical expenditures for facilities transferred to the state.  

[6:0:66,150] The mission of the Judicial Branch is to resolve disputes arising under the law and to interpret and apply the law consistently,  

[6:1:66,160] impartially, and independently to protect the rights and liberties guaranteed by the Constitutions of California and the United  

[6:2:66,171] States, in a fair, accessible, effective, and efficient manner.  

[7:0:66,191] Because the Judicial Branch's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Judicial Branch has a capital outlay  

[8:0:66,201] program to support this need. For   the specifics on   the Judicial Branch's capital outlay program, see "Infrastructure Overview."

[7:0:66,177] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[8:0:65,196] • Trial Court Operations—The Budget includes $184.2 million ongoing General Fund for trial court operations. This amount  

[8:1:76,207] includes $84.2 million, which is the equivalent to a 3.8 percent increase in funding for general trial court operational costs  

[8:2:76,217] statewide to adjust for inflation. The remaining $100 million will be allocated by the Judicial Council to promote fiscal equity  

[8:3:76,227] among the trial courts.  

[9:0:65,243] • Civil Assessment Fees—The Budget reduces the civil assessment fee from a maximum of $300 to $100, deposits civil  

[9:1:76,253] assessments into the General Fund instead of the Trial Court Trust Fund, and includes a total of $67 million ongoing General  

[9:2:76,264] Fund to backfill the Trial Court Trust Fund for the resulting revenue loss. The Budget also includes $10 million one-time  

[9:3:76,274] General Fund to backfill the trial courts for revenue losses resulting from the waiver of past civil assessment debt assessed  

[9:4:76,284] prior to July 1, 2022.  

[10:0:65,300] • Superior Court Judgeships—The Budget includes $39.9 million General Fund in 2022-23 and $39.1 million ongoing for 23  

[10:1:76,310] additional superior court judgeships that are authorized in statute but are currently unfunded. The Budget also includes  

[10:2:76,321] $24.3 million one-time General Fund for facility modifications to accommodate 17 of the 23 judgeships.  

[11:0:65,337] • Trial Court Backfill—The Budget includes $10.3 million ongoing General Fund to backfill trial courts for revenue losses from  

[11:1:76,347] the repeal of certain administrative fees for criminal offenses by Chapter 257, Statutes of 2021 (AB 177), which increases the  

[11:2:76,357] backfill for this purpose to $60.3 million.  

[12:0:65,373] • Filing Fee Waiver—The Budget includes $18 million ongoing General Fund to backfill the Trial Court Trust Fund for revenue  

[12:1:76,384] losses resulting from the expansion of individuals eligible for automatic court filing fee waivers.  

[13:0:65,400] • Community Assistance, Recovery, and Empowerment (CARE) Court—The Budget includes $39.5 million General Fund in  

[13:1:76,410] 2022-23 and $37.7 million ongoing for the Judicial Branch to conduct CARE court hearings and provide resources for self-

[13:2:76,420] help centers, contingent on adoption of statutory changes.  

[14:0:65,436] • Dependency Counsel—The Budget includes $30 million ongoing General Fund for court-appointed counsel for children, non-

[14:1:76,447] minor dependents, and parents in juvenile court dependency proceedings. This increases total funding for this purpose to  

[14:2:76,457] $186.7 million annually.  

[15:0:65,473] • Legal Aid Resources—The Budget includes $30 million one-time General Fund for Legal Aid eviction defense and $15  

[15:1:76,483] million one-time General Fund for a Legal Aid for Consumer Debt Pilot program to assist consumers with debt incurred  

[15:2:76,493] during the pandemic.  

[16:0:65,510] • Court Appointed Special Advocate (CASA) Expansion—The Budget includes $20 million General Fund in 2022-23, 2023-24  

[16:1:76,520] and 2024-25 to expand CASA program capacity, and recruitment and training.  

[17:0:65,536] • Firearm Relinquishment Program—The Budget includes $40 million one-time General Fund to establish a court-based  

[17:1:76,546] firearm relinquishment pilot program in select courts for the safe removal of firearms from individuals prohibited from owning  

[17:2:76,556] or possessing firearms.  

[18:0:65,573] • Branchwide Informational Technology Modernization—The Budget includes $34.7 million General Fund in 2022-23,  

[18:1:76,583] increasing to $40.3 million in 2025-26 and ongoing, for information technology initiatives to modernize the Judicial Branch  

[18:2:76,593] and increase access to justice for the public.  

[19:0:65,609] • Courthouse Lactation Facilities—The Budget includes $15 million one-time General Fund to expand public access to  

[19:1:76,619] lactation facilities in courthouses for court users.  

[20:0:65,636] • Fire, Life, and Safety Trial Court Facility Improvements—The Budget includes $29.7 million one-time General Fund to repair  

[20:1:76,646] existing elevators, escalators, and air condition in the San Diego County Superior Court Hall of Justice. The Budget also  

[20:2:76,656] includes $15.3 million one-time General Fund to address egress deficiencies in the San Diego Superior Court East County  

[20:3:76,666] Regional Center. Lastly, the Budget includes $11.3 million one-time General Fund to address increased project costs for an  

[20:4:76,676] existing fire, life, and safety improvement project in the Orange County Superior Court Central Justice Center.  

[21:0:65,693] • Trial Court Facility Modifications—The Budget includes $15.4 million ongoing General Fund for critical building repairs in the  

[21:1:76,703] trial courts and Courts of Appeal.  

[22:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:65,69] • Judicial Branch Data and Information Governance—The Budget includes $15 million General Fund in 2022-23 and $3.8

[6:1:76,205] and $1.6 million ongoing to implement, support, and maintain remote access to courtroom proceedings pursuant to Chapter  

[6:2:76,215] 526, Statutes of 2021 (AB 716).

[7:1:76,241] and $1.7 million ongoing to implement and support electronic filing interfaces for domestic violence restraining orders,  

[7:2:76,252] domestic temporary restraining orders, and gun violence restraining orders for all trial courts as required by Chapter 681,  

[7:3:76,262] Statutes of 2021 (AB 887) and Chapter 686, Statutes of 2021 (SB 538).  

[8:0:65,278] • Probate Conservatorship Report (AB 1194)—The Budget includes $1.3 million one-time General Fund in 2022-23 for the  

[8:1:76,288] Judicial Council to complete a comprehensive study of probate conservatorships in California and develop statewide  

[8:2:76,298] recommendations pursuant to Chapter 417, Statutes of 2021 (AB 1194).

[3:1:66,85] 0130 - SUPREME COURT  

[4:0:66,105] The Supreme Court is the highest court in the California judicial system. Its decisions are binding on all other California state  

[4:1:66,115] courts. The Chief Justice of California and the six Associate Justices entertain petitions seeking review of decisions from the  

[4:2:66,126] Courts of Appeal, original petitions for extraordinary relief (such as writs of mandate or habeas corpus), and recommendations  

[4:3:66,136] for discipline of judicial officers and attorneys. The Court grants review and issues opinions in order to settle legal questions of  

[4:4:66,146] statewide importance. In addition, under the California Constitution, all death penalty judgments are appealed directly to the  

[4:5:66,156] Supreme Court.  

[4:6:66,174] 0135 - COURTS OF APPEAL  

[5:0:66,194] Established by a constitutional amendment in 1904, the Courts of Appeal are California's intermediate courts of review. The six  

[5:1:66,205] District Courts of Appeal hear appeals and original proceedings at nine different locations around the state. Cases before the  

[5:2:66,215] Courts of Appeal involve every area of civil and criminal law.  

[5:3:66,232] 0140 - JUDICIAL COUNCIL  

[6:0:66,253] The Judicial Council of California is the constitutional policy-making body for the state judiciary. The Council consists of 21  

[6:1:66,263] voting members and 9 advisory members; the Chief Justice of California serves as chair. The Judicial Council staff serve as the  

[6:2:66,273] administrative arm of the Council. Staff provide policy support to the Council, administrative accountability in the operation of  

[6:3:66,283] the courts as specified by law, strategic planning for capital outlay, design, and construction of court facilities; and  

[6:4:66,294] administrative support for courts in areas such as budget, fiscal services, coordination of the assignment of retired judges,  

[6:5:66,304] technology, education, legal advice and services, human resources, legislative advocacy, and research.  

[6:6:66,322] 0140023 - JUDICIAL BRANCH FACILITY PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,339] The Judicial Branch Facility Program administers the acquisition, planning, construction, operations, and maintenance of  

[7:1:66,349] judicial branch facilities. This program is responsible for the development of long-term facilities master plans, facility and real  

[7:2:66,360] estate management, renovation of existing court facilities, and new courthouse planning, design, and construction.  

[7:3:66,377] 0150 - STATE TRIAL COURT FUNDING  

[8:0:66,395] California has 58 trial courts, one in each county. In trial courts, or superior courts, a judge and sometimes a jury hears  

[8:1:66,405] witnesses' testimony and other evidence and decides cases by applying the relevant law to the facts.   Trial courts have trial  

[8:2:66,415] jurisdiction over all criminal and civil cases. To accomplish various statutory mandates, the State Trial Court Funding Program  

[8:3:66,426] supports trial courts through the following programs:  

[8:4:66,446] 0150010 - SUPPORT FOR THE OPERATION OF THE TRIAL COURTS  

[8:5:66,464] This program's objective is to provide the resources necessary for the statewide trial court system to adjudicate civil and  

[8:6:66,474] criminal cases. This program includes all allowable trial court administrative costs under Government Code section 77003.  

[8:7:66,492] 0150011 - COURT APPOINTED DEPENDENCY COUNSEL  

[9:0:66,509] This program provides funding for court-appointed counsel for children, non-minor dependents and parents in juvenile court  

[9:1:66,520] dependency proceedings pursuant to Welfare and Institutions Code section 317, subdivisions (b) and (c), and Government  

[9:2:66,530] Code section 77003, subdivision (a)(4).  

[9:3:66,547] 0150019 - COMPENSATION OF SUPERIOR COURT JUDGES  

[9:4:66,565] This program provides funding for the salaries and state benefits for Superior Court judges.  

[9:5:66,583] 0150028 - ASSIGNED JUDGES  

[10:0:66,601] This program provides support for the salaries and related costs of retired as well as active judges who are assigned by the  

[10:1:66,611] Chief Justice to positions in courts which require assistance due to judicial absences or other factors impacting the ability of a  

[10:2:66,621] court to avoid case delay.  

[10:3:66,639] 0150037 - COURT INTERPRETERS  

[11:0:66,656] This program supports the provision of qualified language interpreters in criminal, juvenile, family and civil proceedings as  

[11:1:66,667] required by statute.  

[11:2:66,684] 0150046 - GRANTS  

[12:0:66,702] This program provides funding for government grants providing public services related to the trial courts.  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] 0150051 – CHILD SUPPORT COMMISSIONER PROGRAM (AB 1058)  

[3:1:66,86] This program provides funding for Child Support Commissioners and Family Law Facilitators in every court.  

[3:2:66,104] 0150067 – COURT APPOINTED SPECIAL ADVOCATE (CASA) PROGRAM  

[4:0:66,122] This program provides funding for non-profits designated by the court to train and provide volunteers to advocate for children in  

[4:1:66,132] foster care.  

[4:2:66,150] 0150083 – EQUAL ACCESS FUND  

[5:0:66,167] This program provides funding for legal services programs to provide a range of services to low-income litigants.  

[5:1:66,185] 0150095 - EXPENSES ON BEHALF OF THE TRIAL COURT  

[6:0:66,203] This program provides appropriation for statewide programs and services provided to the court or courts by the Judicial Council  

[6:1:66,213] or payment for services or property of any kind contracted for the court or courts or on behalf of the courts by the Judicial  

[6:2:66,223] Council, which are paid for or reimbursed by the trial courts. The appropriation also supports the implementation and  

[6:3:66,233] administration of the Civil Representation Pilot Program.  

[7:0:66,251] In addition to the programs mentioned above, the State Trial Court Funding Program supports a number of smaller programs  

[7:1:66,261] including Access to Visitation services in family law proceedings, JusticeCorps volunteers in the courts, and Substance Abuse  

[7:2:66,271] Focus Grants supporting collaborative justice courts.  

[7:3:66,289] 0155 - HABEAS CORPUS RESOURCE CENTER  

[8:0:66,310] The Habeas Corpus Resource Center provides legal representation for indigent petitioners in death penalty habeas corpus  

[8:1:66,320] proceedings before the California state courts   and the federal courts. The Center also recruits and trains attorneys to expand  

[8:2:66,330] the pool of private counsel qualified to accept appointments in death penalty habeas corpus proceedings, serves as a resource  

[8:3:66,340] to them, and thereby helps to reduce the number of unrepresented indigents on California's death row.

[3:0:66,69] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[4:0:66,88] The Judicial Branch consists of the Supreme Court, courts of appeal, trial courts, and the Judicial Council. The Supreme Court  

[4:1:66,98] is located in the 103,300 square foot (sf) Civic Center Plaza in San Francisco and the 7,600 sf Ronald Reagan State Building in  

[4:2:66,109] Los Angeles. The courts of appeal are organized into six districts, operate in nine different locations, and consist of  

[4:3:66,119] approximately 508,000 sf. The trial courts are located in 58 counties statewide, in approximately 450 facilities, and 2,100  

[4:4:66,129] courtrooms of approximately 16 million sf of usable area and more than 21 million sf of space under Judicial Council  

[4:5:66,139] responsibility and management. The facilities of the Supreme Court, courts of appeal, and trial courts encompass not only the  

[4:6:66,149] public courtroom spaces but also the chambers and workspaces where judicial officers and courtroom staff prepare for  

[4:7:66,160] proceedings; secure areas, including holding cells; and building support functions. Judicial Council administrative facilities,  

[4:8:66,170] occupying approximately 263,000 sf, are located in San Francisco and Sacramento.

[7:0:180,434] 0280   Commission on Judicial Performance  

[8:0:66,455] The California Commission on Judicial Performance is the independent state agency responsible for investigating complaints of  

[8:1:66,466] judicial misconduct and judicial incapacity, and for disciplining judges pursuant to Article VI, Section 18 of the California  

[8:2:66,476] Constitution. It is the only body with such authority in the state. Its jurisdiction includes all active judges and justices of  

[8:3:66,486] California's superior courts, Courts of Appeal and Supreme Court, and former judges for conduct prior to retirement or  

[8:4:66,496] resignation. The Commission also shares authority with the local courts for the oversight of court commissioners and referees.  

[8:5:66,506] In addition to its disciplinary functions, the Commission is responsible for handling judges' applications for disability retirement.  

[9:0:66,527] The Commission's authority is limited to investigating alleged judicial misconduct and, if warranted, imposing discipline. Judicial  

[9:1:66,537] misconduct usually involves conduct in conflict with the standards set forth in the Code of Judicial Ethics. After investigation  

[9:2:66,547] and, in some cases a public hearing, the Commission may impose sanctions ranging from confidential discipline to removal  

[9:3:66,557] from office.  

[10:0:66,578] The Commission is composed of 11 members: 3 judges appointed by the Supreme Court; 2 attorneys appointed by the  

[10:1:66,588] Governor; and 6 lay citizens, of which 2 are appointed by the Governor, 2 are appointed by the Senate Committee on Rules,  

[10:2:66,598] and 2 are appointed by the Speaker of the Assembly. Members are appointed to four-year terms and may serve two terms.  

[10:3:66,608] Commission members do not receive a salary.

[5:0:150,140] 0390   Contributions to the Judges' Retirement System  

[6:0:66,162] The Judges' Retirement System provides retirement benefit funding for judges on California's Supreme, Appellate, and  

[6:1:66,172] Superior/Municipal courts. The California Public Employees' Retirement System administers both the Judges' Retirement  

[6:2:66,182] System and the Judges' Retirement System II on behalf of its members.  

[7:0:66,200] The Judges' Retirement System is closed to new members, and a second retirement system for judges was established in  

[7:1:66,210] 1994. All new judges elected or appointed on or after November 9, 1994 become members of the Judges' Retirement System  

[7:2:66,220] II. The Judges' Retirement System II provides retirement, disability, and death benefits based on age, years of service, final  

[7:3:66,231] compensation, and eligibility, as determined by specific sections of the Judges' Retirement System II Law. Judges' Retirement  

[7:4:66,241] System II members eligible for a service retirement have the option of choosing the monetary credit plan (a lump-sum return of  

[7:5:66,251] contributions and interest earned) or the defined benefit plan. Judges' Retirement System II members not eligible for a defined  

[7:6:66,261] benefit receive the balance of their monetary credits (in a lump-sum payment). The Judges' Retirement System II is funded  

[7:7:66,271] through contributions from the state, members of the system, and investment earnings. The state's contributions are adjusted  

[7:8:66,282] annually to maintain actuarial soundness of the fund.  

[8:0:66,302] The Judges' Retirement System provides retirement, disability, and death benefits based on age, years of service,  

[9:0:66,312] compensation of active judges, and eligibility, as determined by specific sections of the Judges' Retirement Law. Active judges  

[9:1:66,322] and the state each contribute 8 percent of salary to the Judges' Retirement System. Additional contributions come from filing  

[9:2:66,333] fees for specific civil cases and investment income. These contributions, however, are not sufficient to fully fund benefit  

[9:3:66,343] payments. Consequently, current law requires the state to fund the difference between existing contribution sources and the  

[9:4:66,353] required benefit payments to retired judges.  

[10:0:66,373] Effective January 1, 2013, provisions of the California Public Employees' Pension Reform Act of 2013, Chapter 296, Statutes of  

[10:1:66,384] 2012 (AB 340), require that new judges contribute 50 percent of the normal cost of their pension benefits.

[2:0:89,32] 0509   Governor's Office of Business and Economic Development (GO-Biz) - 

[2:1:276,47] Continued 

[3:0:66,88] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[4:0:65,107] • Supplemental Paid Sick Leave Relief Grants—The Budget includes $250 million one-time California Emergency Relief Fund  

[4:1:76,118] for relief grants for small businesses and non-profits to offset costs of their employees who have utilized the state’s  

[4:2:76,128] supplemental COVID-19 paid sick leave. The Administration will work with the Legislature over the summer on statutory  

[4:3:76,138] changes to implement this program.  

[5:0:65,154] • California Small Agricultural Business Drought Relief Grant Program—The Budget includes $75 million one-time California  

[5:1:76,164] Emergency Relief Fund to provide direct assistance to eligible agriculture-related businesses that have been affected by  

[5:2:76,175] severe drought conditions. The Administration will work with the Legislature over the summer on statutory changes to  

[5:3:76,185] implement this program.  

[6:0:65,201] • California Regional Initiative for Social Enterprises—The Budget includes $25 million one-time General Fund to establish the  

[6:1:76,211] California Regional Initiative for Social Enterprises Program to provide financial and technical assistance to employment  

[6:2:76,221] social enterprises to accelerate economic mobility and inclusion for individuals that experience employment barriers.  

[7:0:65,238] • Accelerate California: Inclusive Innovation Hub Program—The Budget includes $20 million one-time General Fund, to be  

[7:1:76,248] spent over four years, to expand the number of Hubs from ten to thirteen to align with the Community Economic Resilience  

[7:2:76,258] Fund regions, and to create the Entrepreneurship Fund to provide seed funding to businesses incubated within each of the  

[7:3:76,268] Hubs.  

[8:0:65,284] • California Venues Grant Program—The Budget includes $15 million one-time California Emergency Relief Fund to add and  

[8:1:76,295] fund small movie theaters and drive-ins as part of the California Venues Grant Program.  

[9:0:65,311] • Women's Business Centers—The Budget includes $8 million one-time General Fund, to be spent over three years, to  

[9:1:76,321] support Women’s Business Centers.  

[10:0:65,337] • Technical Assistance Expansion Program and Capital Infusion Program—The Budget includes $6 million General Fund in  

[10:1:76,347] 2022-23 to bolster the Technical Assistance Expansion Program (TAEP) to support the increased demand, and $26 million  

[10:2:76,358] annually after, to permanently fund TAEP ($23 million) and the Capital Infusion Program ($3 million).  

[11:0:65,374] • California Competes (CalCompetes) Grant Program—The Budget includes $120 million one-time General Fund for a second  

[11:1:76,384] year of the CalCompetes Grant Program. The Budget includes provisions for CalCompetes to consider a company's  

[11:2:76,394] willingness to choose California over states that do not protect LGBTQ+ or reproductive rights, and commitment to good  

[11:3:76,404] labor practices, in its awards.  

[12:0:65,421] • California Containerized Ports Interoperability Grant Program—The Budget includes $30 million one-time General Fund to  

[12:1:76,431] provide grants to the five containerized ports to assist in achieving real-time interoperability.  

[13:0:65,447] • Tourism Recovery —The Budget includes $15 million one-time General Fund to implement strategic media recovery  

[13:1:76,457] campaigns that will help continue the recovery of the travel and tourism industry in California. The California Office of  

[13:2:76,467] Tourism, within GO-Biz, will coordinate with Visit California to implement this initiative.  

[14:0:65,484] • Immigration Integration and Economic Development—The Budget includes $11.6 million General Fund in 2022-23, and  

[14:1:76,494] $500,000 annually thereafter, for GO-Biz to coordinate resources and support immigrant integration statewide, enhance  

[14:2:76,504] service provisions for immigrant communities at the local level, and support economic development activities for underserved  

[14:3:76,514] small businesses and entrepreneurs.  

[15:0:65,530] • Sustainable Agriculture Package: Climate Catalyst Fund—The Budget includes $25 million General Fund in 2022-23 as part  

[15:1:76,541] of a $1.1 billion package for investments that support the agriculture sector. This includes funding for programs that create a  

[15:2:76,551] healthy, resilient, and equitable food system; promote climate smart agriculture to support long-term sustainability and  

[15:3:76,561] resilience; and address economic recovery and high-road job growth.  

[16:0:65,577] • Local Government Budget Sustainability Fund—The Budget includes $300 million General Fund ($100 million in 2023-24,  

[16:1:76,587] $100 million in 2024-25, and $100 million in 2025-26) to establish the Local Government Budget Sustainability Fund to  

[16:2:76,598] provide grants to counties facing significant challenges to their near-term sustainability that demonstrate a clear commitment  

[16:3:76,608] to advancing a more climate-resilient local economy. The Administration will work with the Legislature over the summer on  

[16:4:76,618] statutory changes to implement this program.

[6:1:66,693] business development, private sector investment, and economic growth, and export promotion. This program makes

[6:3:66,713] economic goals.

[3:0:66,69] 0225 - CALIFORNIA BUSINESS INVESTMENT SERVICES  

[4:0:66,86] This program serves employers, corporate executives, business owners, and site location consultants who are considering  

[4:1:66,97] California for business investment and expansion. This program convenes teams on key business development issues. This  

[4:2:66,107] program works with local, state, and federal partners to attract, retain, and grow businesses in addition to providing permit  

[4:3:66,117] assistance and helping businesses succeed in California.  

[5:0:66,135] 0230 - CALIFORNIA OFFICE OF THE SMALL BUSINESS ADVOCATE  

[6:0:66,152] The California Office of the Small Business Advocate (CalOSBA) serves as the principal advocate in the state on behalf of  

[6:1:66,163] small businesses, including legislation and administrative regulations that affect small business. CalOSBA ensures that  

[6:2:66,173] information, content and direct support are available to minority and small businesses to better navigate state resources,  

[6:3:66,183] programs and services. CalOSBA advocates on behalf of small businesses concerning the actions of state agencies, state  

[6:4:66,193] laws, regulations and procurement opportunities, maintaining the connection to agency small business advocates/liaisons. It  

[6:5:66,203] serves as the co-Coordinator with CalOES for the Economic Recovery Support Function under the California Disaster  

[6:6:66,214] Recovery Framework. CalOSBA administers the Technical Assistance Expansion Program and Capital Infusion Program grants  

[6:7:66,224] for support services to underserved business groups, as well as the Innovation Hub (iHub) and CA Made programs.  

[7:0:66,241] 0235010 - CALIFORNIA FILM COMMISSION  

[8:0:66,259] The California Film Commission (CFC) enhances California's status as the leader in motion picture, television and commercial  

[8:1:66,269] production. The Commission is tasked with retaining and increasing motion picture production and to see that it continues to  

[8:2:66,280] create jobs and boost business throughout the State. A one-stop office for filmmakers, the Commission supports productions of  

[8:3:66,290] all sizes and budgets with a variety of services. In addition to issuing film permits for all state properties, administering the film  

[8:4:66,300] and TV tax credit program, maintaining an extensive location library, and offering production assistance on a wide variety of  

[8:5:66,310] issues, CFC also works closely with cities and counties with the goal of creating 'film friendly" policies that are consistent state-

[8:6:66,320] wide.  

[9:0:66,338] 0235019 - DIVISION OF TOURISM  

[10:0:66,356] The California Tourism Market Act provides for the marketing of California through an assessment of businesses that benefit  

[10:1:66,366] from travel and tourism. The objective of the Tourism Assessment Program is to identify potentially assessable businesses,  

[10:2:66,376] assist companies with determining the appropriate amount of their self-assessment, and collet the fee.  

[11:0:66,394] 0235028 - CALIFORINIA INFRASTRUCTURE AND ECONOMIC DEVELOPMENT BANK  

[12:0:66,412] California Infrastructure and Economic Development Bank (IBank) was created to finance public infrastructure and private  

[12:1:66,422] development that promote a healthy climate for jobs, contribute to a strong economy and improve the quality of life in California  

[12:2:66,432] communities. IBank has broad authority to participate in a variety of financing transactions, including issuing tax-exempt and  

[12:3:66,442] taxable revenue bonds, providing financing to a variety of entities, providing credit enhancements, acquiring or leasing facilities,  

[12:4:66,452] and leveraging State and Federal funds. IBank's current programs include the infrastructure state revolving fund program (ISRF  

[12:5:66,463] Program), the California lending for energy and environmental needs center (CLEEN Center, a sub-program of the ISRF  

[12:6:66,473] Program), the statewide energy and efficiency program and the light emitting diode street lighting program (each a component  

[12:7:66,483] of the CLEEN Center), the bond financing program, which issues 501(c)(3)tax-exempt and taxable bonds,, industrial  

[12:8:66,493] development revenue bonds, exempt facility revenue bonds, and public agency revenue bonds.  

[13:0:66,511] 0235037 - SMALL BUSINESS LOAN GUARANTEE PROGRAM  

[14:0:66,529] The Small Business Loan Guarantee Program (SBLGP) promotes local economic development by providing guarantees for  

[14:1:66,539] loans issued to small businesses from financial institutions, typically banks, that otherwise would not approve a term loan or  

[14:2:66,549] line of credit to a small business. As a result of the SBLGP, participating small businesses are able to secure financing that  

[14:3:66,559] allows them to grow and expand their business. The loan guarantee serves as a credit enhancement and an incentive for  

[14:4:66,569] financial institutions to make loans to small businesses that otherwise would not be eligible for such financing.  

[15:0:66,587] 0235046 - CALIFORNIA WELCOME CENTERS  

[16:0:66,605] California Welcome Centers are visitor information centers that are readily accessible to and recognizable by tourists to  

[16:1:66,615] encourage tourism in California and provide benefits to the state economy. The objective of the California Welcome Center  

[16:2:66,625] Program is to determine the locality of underserved travelers, designate a welcome center, and establish operating standards  

[16:3:66,635] across the network.  

[17:0:66,653] 0240 - CALIFORNIA COMMUNITY REINVESTMENT GRANT PROGRAM 

[18:0:66,671] The California Community Reinvestment Grants (CalCRG) Program   was established on July 1, 2018   to   implement the  

[18:1:66,681] competitive grant program mandated by Proposition 64, the Adult Use of Marijuana Act, and the provisions specified in  

[18:2:66,691] Revenue and Taxation Code section 34019(d).   The CalCRG Program administers grants   for   local public   health departments  

[18:3:66,701] and qualified community-based nonprofit organizations to support various mental health treatment, substance use treatment,  

[18:4:66,712] job placement, legal services and other health and wellness-related programs for communities disproportionately affected by  

[19:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[7:3:66,319] services and state-licensed financial institutions; enforcement of the sale of alcoholic beverages and cannabis; regulation of the  

[7:4:66,329] horse racing industry; protection of civil rights; homelessness prevention through the coordination of funding and services; and  

[7:5:66,340] safe and affordable housing opportunities. The Agency is comprised of the following entities: the Department of Alcoholic  

[7:6:66,350] Beverage Control, the Department of Cannabis Control, the Department of Consumer Affairs, the Department of Financial  

[7:7:66,360] Protection and Innovation, the Department of Real Estate, the California Housing Finance Agency, the Department of Housing  

[7:8:66,370] and Community Development, the Department of Fair Employment and Housing, the Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals  

[7:9:66,380] Board, the Cannabis Control Appeals Panel, the California Horse Racing Board, and the California Homeless Coordinating and  

[7:10:66,391] Financing Council.   In addition, the Agency is charged with administration of the California Interagency Council on  

[7:11:66,401] Homelessness, previously named the Homeless Coordinating and Financing Council.

[5:0:66,141] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,160] 0280 - SECRETARY FOR HEALTH AND HUMAN SERVICES  

[6:0:66,180] The Secretary for California Health and Human Services Agency provides the Governor with the highest level of advice on  

[6:1:66,190] state health, human services and related budget policy issues. The Secretary also provides the highest level of leadership and  

[6:2:66,201] oversight of the agency-wide efforts to promote the health and well-being of a growing and increasingly diverse California  

[6:3:66,211] population.  

[7:0:66,229] 0285 - THE CALIFORNIA OFFICE OF HEALTH INFORMATION INTEGRITY  

[8:0:66,249] The California Office of Health Information Integrity (CalOHII) is responsible for statewide leadership, coordination, policy  

[8:1:66,259] formation, direction, and oversight responsibilities for federal Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA)  

[8:2:66,269] requirements. As the leading resource for providing HIPAA expertise to the State of California, CalOHII exercises full authority  

[8:3:66,280] relative to state entities to establish policy, provide direction to state entities, monitor progress and report on implementation  

[8:4:66,290] efforts based on state and federal laws.  

[8:5:66,307] 0286 - OFFICE OF YOUTH AND COMMUNITY RESTORATION  

[9:0:66,325] The mission for the Office of Youth and Community Restoration is to support the transition of justice involved youth to be  

[9:1:66,335] served in local communities and promote trauma responsive, culturally informed services for youth involved in the juvenile  

[9:2:66,346] justice system that support the youths’ successful transition into adulthood and help them become responsible, thriving, and  

[9:3:66,356] engaged members of their communities.  

[9:4:66,373] 0290 - OFFICE OF SYSTEMS INTEGRATION  

[10:0:66,394] The Office of Systems Integration's (OSI) mission is to procure, manage, and deliver complex technology systems that support  

[10:1:66,404] the delivery of health and human services for automation projects for the Health and Human Services Agency, the Department  

[10:2:66,414] of Social Services, the Department of Health Care Services, the Department of Public Health, and the Health Benefit  

[10:3:66,424] Exchange, including: 

[11:0:65,442] • California Healthcare Eligibility, Enrollment and Retention System (CalHEERS)  

[11:1:65,458] • Case Management, Information and Payrolling System II (CMIPS)  

[11:2:65,475] • Child Welfare Services/Case Management System (CWS/CMS)  

[11:3:65,491] • Child Welfare Digital Services (CWDS)  

[12:0:65,507] • Child Welfare Services - California Automated Response and Engagement System  

[12:1:65,523] • Electronic Benefit Transfer System (EBT)  

[12:2:65,539] • Statewide Automated Welfare System (SAWS)  

[12:3:65,556] • Welfare Data Tracking Implementation Project (WDTIP)  

[12:4:66,573] 0295 - OFFICE OF THE PATIENT ADVOCATE  

[13:0:66,591] The mission of the Office of the Patient Advocate is to improve the access to and quality of health care services by promoting  

[13:1:66,601] transparency and accountability. The Office of the Patient Advocate is mandated to produce a number of public reports on  

[13:2:66,611] health care service topics. Current reporting activities include:  

[13:3:65,629] • Online Annual Health Care Quality Report Cards on health plans and medical groups,  

[13:4:65,645] • A Baseline Review and annual report on the State Consumer Assistance Call Centers and health care complaint data,  

[13:5:65,661] • Online Timely Access Compliance report on California Department of Managed Health Care health care services plans and  

[13:6:76,672] providers, and  

[14:0:65,688] • Model protocols for State Consumer Assistance Call Centers.  

[14:1:66,706] 0296 - CENTER FOR DATA INSIGHTS AND INNOVATION  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] The mission of the Center for Data Insights and Innovation is to improve the lives of all Californians by turning data into

[3:2:66,89] decision making, and integrated care and services.  

[3:3:66,107] 0297 - OFFICE OF THE SURGEON GENERAL  

[4:0:66,124] The position of the California Surgeon General was established to bring heightened attention to upstream factors that influence  

[4:1:66,135] chronic and acute conditions, advise the Governor, and serve as a leading spokesperson on matters of public health.  

[4:2:66,145] Specifically, the office is responsible for (a) Raising public awareness on and coordinating policies governing scientific  

[4:3:66,155] screening and treatment for toxic stress and adverse childhood experiences (ACEs); (b) Advising the Governor, the Secretary  

[4:4:66,165] of the California Health and Human Services Agency, and policymakers on a comprehensive approach to address health  

[4:5:66,175] issues and challenges focused on health equity, early childhood development, and ACEs and toxic stress; (c) Marshalling the  

[4:6:66,186] insights and energy of medical professionals, scientists, and other academic and public health experts, public servants, and  

[4:7:66,196] everyday Californians to solve our most pressing health challenges.

[8:0:164,441] 0540   Secretary of the Natural Resources Agency  

[9:0:66,462] The mission of the Natural Resources Agency is to restore, protect and manage the state's natural, historical and cultural  

[9:1:66,472] resources for current and future generations using creative approaches and solutions based on science, collaboration and  

[9:2:66,482] respect for all involved communities. The Secretary for Natural Resources, a member of the Governor's Cabinet, sets the  

[9:3:66,493] policies and coordinates the environmental preservation and restoration activities of 27 various departments, boards,  

[9:4:66,503] commissions and conservancies, and directly administers the Sea Grant Program, Ocean Protection Council, California  

[9:5:66,513] Environmental Quality Act, Environmental Enhancement Mitigation Program, River Parkways, Urban Greening, and the  

[9:6:66,523] California Cultural and Historical Endowment grant programs.  

[10:0:66,544] The Natural Resources Agency consists of the departments of Forestry and Fire Protection, Conservation, Fish and Wildlife,  

[10:1:66,554] Parks and Recreation, and Water Resources; the California Conservation Corps; Exposition Park; California Science Center;  

[10:2:66,564] California African American Museum; Office of Energy Infrastructure Safety; the State Lands Commission; the Colorado River  

[10:3:66,574] Board; the San Francisco Bay Conservation and Development Commission; the Energy Resources Conservation and  

[10:4:66,584] Development Commission; the Wildlife Conservation Board; the Delta Protection Commission; the California Coastal  

[11:0:66,595] Commission; the State Coastal Conservancy; the California Tahoe Conservancy; the Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy;  

[11:1:66,605] the Coachella Valley Mountains Conservancy; the San Joaquin River Conservancy; the San Gabriel and Lower Los Angeles  

[11:2:66,615] Rivers and Mountains Conservancy; the Baldwin Hills Conservancy; the San Diego River Conservancy; the Sierra Nevada  

[11:3:66,625] Conservancy; the Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta Conservancy; the Native American Heritage Commission; and the Special  

[11:4:66,635] Resources Program.

[8:2:76,604] General Fund in 2023-24, as part of a $5.2 billion package to support water resilience projects that improve environmental  

[8:3:76,615] conditions.  

[9:0:65,631] • Climate Resilience Package—The Budget includes $150 million General Fund in 2022-23, and $150 million General Fund in  

[9:1:76,641] 2023-24, as part of a $3.7 billion package of investments that address the state’s climate risks. This includes funding to  

[9:2:76,651] support urban greening projects and to enhance ocean protection and coastal resilience.  

[10:0:65,667] • Wildfire and Forest Resilience Package—The Budget includes $15 million General Fund in 2022-23 for stewardship of state-

[10:1:76,678] owned land as part of a $1.2 billion package of investments that target the state’s wildfire risks. Of the total $1.2   billion,  

[10:2:76,688] $530   million will be allocated at a later time.  

[11:0:65,704] • California Climate Information System (CalCIS)—The Budget includes $18.3 million General Fund in 2022-23, to support the  

[11:1:76,714] initial planning and development of CalCIS, which will integrate and analyze the most current climate monitoring data to

[12:0:199,530] 0552   Office of the Inspector General  

[13:0:66,552] The Office of the Inspector General (OIG) protects public safety by safeguarding the integrity of California's correctional  

[13:1:66,562] system. OIG is responsible for contemporaneous oversight of the California Department of Corrections and Rehabilitation's  

[13:2:66,572] (CDCR) internal affairs investigations, use of force, staff complaints, and the employee disciplinary process. The Inspector  

[13:3:66,582] General audits and reviews the policies, practices, and procedures of CDCR. The Inspector General reviews the Governor's  

[13:4:66,593] candidates for appointment to serve as warden for the state's adult correctional institutions and as superintendents for the  

[13:5:66,603] state's juvenile facilities, and conducts metric-oriented inspection programs to periodically review delivery of medical care at  

[13:6:66,613] each state prison. OIG receives communications from individuals alleging improper governmental activity and maintains a toll-

[13:7:66,623] free public telephone number to receive allegations of wrongdoing by employees of CDCR; conducts formal reviews of  

[13:8:66,633] complaints of retaliation from CDCR employees against upper management where a legally cognizable cause of action is  

[13:9:66,644] present; and reviews sexual abuse incidents within correctional institutions. OIG provides critical public transparency for the  

[13:10:66,654] state correctional system by publicly reporting its findings.  

[14:0:66,672] In addition, OIG is responsible for the California Rehabilitation Oversight Board (C-ROB) that examines CDCR's various mental  

[14:1:66,682] health, substance abuse, educational, and employment programs for incarcerated individuals and parolees. C-ROB meets at  

[14:2:66,692] least twice annually to recommend modifications, additions, and eliminations of rehabilitation and treatment programs. C-ROB  

[14:3:66,702] also submits biannual reports to the Governor, the Legislature, and the public to convey its findings on the effectiveness of  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[5:0:175,140] 0555   Secretary for Environmental Protection  

[6:0:66,162] The Secretary for Environmental Protection is the cabinet-level agency responsible for protecting the environment. California  

[7:0:66,172] Environmental Protection Agency programs reduce greenhouse gas emissions, restore and protect environmental quality, and  

[7:1:66,182] protect public health. The Secretary coordinates the state's environmental regulatory programs and oversees the fair and  

[8:0:66,192] consistent enforcement of environmental law, which safeguards the state's residents and promotes the state's economic vitality.  

[8:1:66,203] The Secretary also administers environmental justice, environmental law enforcement, emergency preparedness and  

[9:0:66,213] response, Certified Unified Program Agencies, and scientific review programs. The Secretary leads greenhouse gas emission  

[9:1:66,223] reduction and climate change activities in state government, and is responsible for coordinating the implementation of Chapter  

[9:2:66,233] 488, Statutes of 2006 (AB 32). The Secretary also centralizes coordination of California-Mexico border environmental efforts.

[7:1:66,478] 0340 - SUPPORT PROGRAM

[8:1:66,509] environmental quality, and protecting public health while fostering economic vitality. The Secretary oversees the state's

[8:4:66,539] and objectively measuring and reporting results.

[9:0:195,433] 0650   Office of Planning and Research  

[10:0:66,455] The Office of Planning and Research (OPR) assists the Governor and the Administration in planning, research, policy  

[10:1:66,465] development, and legislative analysis. OPR formulates long-range state goals and policies to address four key areas: land use  

[10:2:66,475] and planning, climate risk and resilience, sustainable economic development, and targeted long-range research needs. OPR  

[10:3:66,485] acts as the state's liaison to a variety of entities including local and regional government, planning professionals, small  

[10:4:66,496] business, and the Department of Defense. OPR also serves a role as convener across multiple agencies on cross-cutting  

[10:5:66,506] issues in the four key areas of our work.  

[11:0:66,526] The Office of Planning and Research includes three main budget programs: the State Policy Program (often referred to as  

[11:1:66,536] OPR), which houses most of core planning and research functions; the California Strategic Growth Council, which coordinates  

[11:2:66,547] and works collaboratively with public agencies, communities, and stakeholders to realize healthy, thriving, resilient communities  

[11:3:66,557] for all and provides direct grant funding to projects demonstrating the value and feasibility of sustainable communities; and  

[11:4:66,567] California Volunteers, a program aimed to increase the number and impact of Californians involved with service and  

[11:5:66,577] volunteering throughout the state.

[6:1:65,417] • Climate Resilience Package (SB 170)—The Budget includes $364.7 million General Fund in 2022-23, and $399.7 million  

[6:2:76,427] General Fund in 2023-24 as part of a $3.7 billion package of investments to address the state’s climate risks included in  

[6:3:76,437] Chapter 240, Statutes of 2021 (SB 170). This includes funding to support the California Climate Action Corps State Service  

[6:4:76,447] Program, regional climate adaptation strategy development and implementation, and the Vulnerable Communities Platform  

[6:5:76,458] and Cal-Adapt Mapping platforms.  

[7:0:65,474] • Carnegie Science Climate Research Hub Facility—The Budget includes $20 million one-time General Fund for a grant to the  

[7:1:76,484] Carnegie Science Climate Research Hub facility in Pasadena.  

[8:0:65,500] • CEQANet 2.0 System Maintenance and Redesign—The Budget includes $1.3 million General Fund in 2022-23, and $48,000  

[8:1:76,510] in 2023-24 and ongoing to host and maintain the current California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA) document online  

[8:2:76,521] processing system, and to provide planning resources for a system redesign.  

[9:0:65,537] • California Environmental Quality Act Workload (AB 819)—The Budget includes $374,000 General Fund in 2022-23, and  

[9:1:76,547] $224,000 ongoing to modernize and digitize the CEQA filing process pursuant to Chapter 97, Statutes of 2021 (AB 819).  

[9:2:65,563] • Forestry and Wildfire Resilience—The Budget includes $219,000 ongoing General Fund for one position to coordinate  

[9:3:76,573] planning and implementation related to wildfire risk mitigation pursuant to various enacted legislation.  

[10:0:65,590] • Coastal Resource: Sea Level Rise (SB 1)—The Budget includes $174,000 ongoing General Fund to support development of  

[10:1:76,600] guidelines for the identification, assessment, minimization, and mitigation of sea level rise pursuant to Chapter 236, Statutes  

[10:2:76,610] of 2021 (SB 1).  

[11:0:65,626] • Strategic Growth Council: California Jobs Plan Act of 2021 (AB 680)—The Budget includes $574,000 ongoing Greenhouse  

[11:1:76,636] Gas Reduction Fund to support the California Air Resources Board to update Greenhouse Gas Reduction Fund grant  

[11:2:76,647] guidelines and eligibility, and support grant applicants connecting with workforce development resources in their region  

[11:3:76,657] pursuant to Chapter 746, Statutes of 2021 (AB 680).  

[12:0:65,673] • Strategic Growth Council: Affordable Rental and Owner-Occupied Housing: Equity in State and Local Programs (AB  

[13:0:76,683] 1095)—The Budget includes $158,000 ongoing General Fund to promulgate and enact eligibility criteria that includes both  

[13:1:76,693] affordable housing rental units and owner-occupied affordable housing units as projects permitted to receive grants under  

[13:2:76,704] the Affordable Housing and Sustainable Communities Program pursuant to Chapter 746, Statutes of 2021 (AB 1095).

[7:0:66,605] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:1:66,624] 0360 - STATE PLANNING AND POLICY DEVELOPMENT  

[8:0:66,642] By statute, OPR is the state comprehensive planning agency. The major activities of the State Planning and Policy  

[8:1:66,652] Development program can be grouped into four major categories: land use and planning, climate risk and resilience,  

[8:2:66,662] sustainable economic development, and targeted long-range research needs. More specifically these include: (1)  

[8:3:66,673] recommending and implementing state policies on land-use and growth planning and compliance with environmental statutes  

[8:4:66,683] and regulations, including General Plan Guidelines and other guidance to local and regional governments; (2) coordinating the  

[8:5:66,693] implementation of the California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA), including: (a) operating the State Clearinghouse; (b)  

[8:6:66,703] maintaining an online database for the electronic submission and archiving of CEQA documents; (c) developing CEQA  

[8:7:66,713] guidelines amendments; (d) providing technical assistance; (e) providing training, and (f) coordinating SB 7 project review (3)  

[9:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[5:2:66,262] broadly accessible jobs; and (16) Conducting other activities as the Governor may direct.  

[6:0:66,280] 0365 - CALIFORNIA VOLUNTEERS  

[7:0:66,298] California Volunteers is the state office tasked with engaging Californians in services, volunteering and civic action to tackle our  

[7:1:66,308] state’s most pressing challenges while lifting up all communities. From the California Climate Action Corps, the country’s first  

[7:2:66,318] statewide climate corps program, to the #CaliforniansForAll service initiative launched in response to COVID-19 to establish a  

[7:3:66,328] volunteer corps to support the state’s response to emergencies and disasters, California Volunteers plays an integral role in  

[7:4:66,339] tackling the state’s problems. California Volunteers is led by the State’s Chief Service Officer and supported by a bipartisan  

[7:5:66,349] 25-member Commission and a team of experts driven by a mission to empower and mobilize all Californians to volunteer and  

[7:6:66,359] serve in their communities.  

[8:0:66,377] 0370 - CALIFORNIA STRATEGIC GROWTH COUNCIL  

[9:0:66,394] The California Strategic Growth Council (SGC) coordinates and works collaboratively with public agencies, communities, and  

[9:1:66,405] stakeholders to advance healthy, thriving, and resilient communities for all. SGC integrates State, regional, and local efforts to  

[9:2:66,415] reduce greenhouse gas emissions; increase the availability of affordable housing located near jobs, services, and green space;  

[9:3:66,425] conserve agriculture lands; strengthen community resilience; promote public health; and advance racial equity. SGC  

[9:4:66,435] administers the following grant programs: Affordable Housing and Sustainable Communities; Transformative Climate  

[10:0:66,445] Communities; Sustainable Agricultural Lands Conservation; Climate Change Research – all of which are part of the California  

[10:1:66,456] Climate Investments Program, funded by proceeds from the State’s cap-and-trade auction revenues. SGC operates these  

[10:2:66,466] funding programs in collaboration with interagency teams to develop program guidelines, review applications, and administer  

[10:3:66,476] program funds. SGC’s Community Assistance for Climate Equity Program encompasses both the Regional Climate  

[10:4:66,486] Collaboratives, which supports local capacity building and technical assistance in under-resourced communities across  

[11:0:66,496] California; and the California Climate Investments Technical Assistance Program, which increases access to SGC’s own grant  

[11:1:66,507] programs as well as those of many other State agencies. SGC partners with High-Speed Rail Authority (HSRA) to support  

[11:2:66,517] sustainable development and conservation practices, especially in future HSR station areas. SGC’s Health and Equity Program  

[11:3:66,527] advances the integration of health and equity principles in State programs and planning through the Health in All Policies Task  

[11:4:66,537] Force, partnerships and collaborations, and in the implementation of SGC’s Racial Equity Action Plan. SGC's Community  

[11:5:66,547] Resilience Centers Program provides grants to support communities' ability to withstand climate-related disasters and build  

[11:6:66,558] resilience year-round. Finally, SGC is charged with reviewing the State’s 5-Year Infrastructure Plan and to contribute to  

[11:7:66,568] interagency efforts to promote sustainability, equity, and resilience in State investments.  

[12:0:66,586] 0371 - OFFICE OF COMMUNITY PARTNERSHIPS AND STRATEGIC COMMUNICATIONS  

[13:0:66,603] The Office of Community Partnerships and Strategic Communications (OCPSC) manages the State’s highest priority  

[14:0:66,613] community engagement and public awareness efforts and will be initially focused on COVID-19 vaccines and climate justice  

[14:1:66,624] such as water conservation. OCPSC works with respective state agencies and departments to inform outreach campaign  

[14:2:66,634] efforts and support greater coordination and collaboration within state government. OCPSC recognizes the vital role public-

[14:3:66,644] private partners and community partners play in helping to mobilize coordinated outreach efforts to address the State’s most  

[14:4:66,654] pressing issues. In partnership with an ecosystem of trusted messengers, including ethnic media partners, OCPSC directly  

[14:5:66,664] engages Californians, including those experiencing the greatest health and social inequities, with culturally competent and  

[14:6:66,675] actionable information.

[12:0:202,544] 0690   Office of Emergency Services  

[13:0:66,565] The principal objective of the Office of Emergency Services (Cal OES) is to protect lives and property, build emergency  

[13:1:66,575] response capabilities, and support communities for a resilient California. The Cal OES Director serves as both the Governor's  

[13:2:66,586] Homeland Security Advisor and Emergency Management Director, with responsibility to implement and facilitate the state's  

[13:3:66,596] homeland security and counter-terrorism strategy. Cal OES serves the public through effective collaboration in preparing for,  

[13:4:66,606] protecting against, responding to, recovering from, and mitigating the impacts of hazards and threats.  

[13:5:66,624] Cal OES provides leadership, assistance, training, and support to state and local agencies and coordinates with federal  

[13:6:66,634] agencies in planning and preparing for the most effective use of federal, state, local, and private sector resources in  

[13:7:66,644] emergencies. This emergency planning is based upon a system of mutual aid whereby a jurisdiction relies first on its own  

[13:8:66,654] resources, and then requests assistance from its neighbors. Cal OES' plans and programs are coordinated with those of the  

[13:9:66,664] federal government, other states, private sector, utilities, and state and local agencies within California.  

[13:10:66,682] During an emergency, Cal OES functions as the Governor's immediate staff to provide guidance and coordinate the state's  

[13:11:66,692] responsibilities under the Emergency Services Act and applicable federal statutes. It also acts as the conduit for federal  

[13:12:66,703] assistance through natural disaster grants and federal agency support. Additionally, Cal OES is responsible for the  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 124 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] 2022-23 STATE BUDGET — LJE   122 

[1:0:382,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:167,32] 0690   Office of Emergency Services - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] development and coordination of a comprehensive state strategy related to all hazards, including terrorism, which includes  

[3:1:66,79] prevention, preparedness, mitigation, response, and recovery.  

[4:0:66,97] Cal OES implements the state's homeland security strategy by overseeing the California Cybersecurity Integration Center and  

[4:1:66,107] the State Threat Assessment System (STAS), which includes management of the State Threat Assessment Center. The STAS  

[4:2:66,117] assists in the detection, prevention, and investigation of   and response to criminal and terrorist activity and   also facilitates  

[4:3:66,127] information and intelligence sharing between local, state, federal, tribal agencies, and private sector partners.  

[5:0:66,145] The Alfred E. Alquist Seismic Safety   Commission   is a separate unit within the   Office of Emergency Services. Its mission is to  

[5:1:66,155] lower earthquake risk to life and property of Californians. The Commission works with federal, state, and local agencies, as well  

[5:2:66,165] as the private sector, on a variety of activities that guide and stimulate earthquake risk reduction and management.  

[6:0:66,183] Because Cal OES' programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, Cal OES has a capital outlay program to support this  

[6:1:66,193] need. For the specifics on Cal OES' capital outlay program, see "Infrastructure Overview."

[7:0:66,207] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[8:0:74,226] ◦ Safeguarding the Availability and Delivery of Emergency Supplies and Commodities—The Budget includes $114.3 million  

[8:1:85,236] General Fund one-time to provide warehouse space, purchase new and replace expiring personal protective equipment,  

[8:2:85,247] increase commodity supply for an all-hazard event, and secure logistic support equipment. This proposal will support  

[8:3:85,257] California’s ability to act quickly and help ensure appropriate resources are available across the state for all emergencies.  

[9:0:74,273] ◦ Nonprofit Security Grant Program—The Budget includes $50 million General Fund one-time to provide security assistance  

[9:1:85,283] to nonprofit organizations at risk of hate-motivated violence, which includes members of the Asian American Pacific  

[9:2:85,293] Islander, LGBTQ+, Black, and Jewish communities. Security enhancement projects include reinforced doors and gates,  

[9:3:85,304] high-intensity lighting and alarms, and other security-related improvements.  

[10:0:74,320] ◦ Fire Integrated Real-Time Intelligence System—The Budget includes $30 million ongoing General Fund, approximately  

[10:1:85,330] $24 million in Cal OES's budget, to establish   the Fire Integrated Real-Time Intelligence System (FIRIS) program  

[10:2:85,340] to   increase the real-time information and situational awareness available to the state and   California’s   mutual aid system  

[10:3:85,350] responders and managers on all hazards events, including wildfires. FIRIS will utilize a combination of aircraft, high-

[10:4:85,361] definition real time video, and data analysis dispatched   24/7   throughout the state to provide nearly instantaneous,  

[10:5:85,371] accurate visual situation awareness that informs public safety and emergency management operational and   tactical  

[10:6:85,381] decisions.   Early detection and rapid intelligence   allows   for the strategic and efficient allocation of resources   before,  

[10:7:85,391] during,   and   after   disaster incidents, and a more effective response to reduce loss of life and property. The intelligence  

[10:8:85,401] gathered will   also provide data and   support   to the Wildfire Forecast and Threat Intelligence Integration Center, as  

[10:9:85,412] established through SB 209 (Chapter   405, Statutes   of   2019).  

[11:0:74,428] ◦ Responding to the Pandemic—The Budget includes $29.5 million General Fund one-time to continue COVID-19 testing  

[11:1:85,438] efforts and provide resources to activate the state’s mutual aid system in the event of future outbreaks. 

[12:0:74,454] ◦ Providing Local Law Enforcement Mutual Aid Deployment Resources—The Budget includes $25 million General Fund for  

[12:1:85,464] three years and five positions to provide local law enforcement support during disasters and emergencies. This funding  

[12:2:85,475] will reduce local costs, accelerate response time, and provide sustainable participation of all 58 counties within the State  

[12:3:85,485] Law Enforcement Mutual Aid system by minimizing funding considerations from the decision-making process.  

[13:0:74,501] ◦ California Earthquake Early Warning System—The Budget includes $17.1 million ongoing General Fund to support  

[14:0:85,511] education and outreach, operations, and research and development of the California Earthquake Early Warning Program.  

[14:1:85,521] This funding will allow the state to increase its earthquake sensor density in the rural parts of Northern California and the  

[14:2:85,532] Sierra Nevada Microwave Telemetry project, and offer grants to community-based organizations to educate socially  

[14:3:85,542] isolated groups and other under-served communities. Cal OES will also undertake new research in the use of Frequency  

[14:4:85,552] Modulation radio frequencies, television broadcasting networks, and crowd sourcing for expanded modalities of alerting  

[14:5:85,562] the public when a significant earthquake occurs.  

[15:0:74,578] ◦ Enhancing the Fire and Rescue Mutual Aid Fire Fleet—The Budget includes $11.2 million General Fund ($10.9 million  

[15:1:85,589] ongoing) and 11 positions to enhance the California State Fire and Rescue Mutual Aid System’s fire fleet. The Fire and  

[15:2:85,599] Rescue Division provides federal, state, and local firefighting and rescue surge capacity. Given the severity and increased  

[15:3:85,609] frequency of wildfires in California, the potential for earthquakes, floods, mud and debris flows, and hazardous materials  

[15:4:85,619] incidents, these resources will enhance the capacity of the mutual aid system to meet the demands of the state’s disaster  

[15:5:85,629] response entities.  

[16:0:74,646] ◦ 988 Suicide Hotline Implementation—The Budget includes $7.5 million General Fund ($6 million ongoing) and 10  

[17:0:85,656] positions to implement a new federally mandated 988 call system to increase the ease and accessibility to provide help for  

[17:1:85,666] those considering suicide or self-harm.   Those who dial 988 will be directed to one of the existing 13 Lifeline Call Centers  

[17:2:85,676] across the state.   These centers already provide immediate assistance with mental distress, and the additional funding will  

[17:3:85,686] ensure those needing services receive help more efficiently.   The Budget includes funding for call handling equipment so  

[17:4:85,697] existing crisis hotline centers have the needed resources to process additional 988 calls and coordinate and transfer calls  

[17:5:85,707] with no loss of information between the 988 and 911 systems.  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,66] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[4:0:66,85] 0380 - EMERGENCY MANAGEMENT SERVICES  

[5:0:66,105] Working through the state's mutual aid system, the Emergency Management Services and Homeland Security Program  

[5:1:66,115] coordinates the provision   of federal, state, and local resources to jurisdictions whose resources and services are overextended  

[5:2:66,126] in a disaster situation. In addition, this program develops and coordinates a comprehensive state strategy related to an all-

[5:3:66,136] hazards approach that includes prevention, preparedness, mitigation, response, and recovery consistent with the National  

[5:4:66,146] Strategy on Homeland Security. Central to this effort is maintaining operational readiness at all levels of government. Within this  

[5:5:66,156] program, Cal OES also trains emergency managers, responders, and state and local leaders in preparedness and response to  

[5:6:66,166] all hazards.  

[6:0:66,184] 0385 - SPECIAL PROGRAMS AND GRANT MANAGEMENT  

[7:0:66,202] Cal OES serves as the State Administering Agent for federal homeland security, emergency management, and victim services  

[7:1:66,212] grants and is the primary liaison with the Federal Emergency Management Agency, the United States Department of Homeland  

[7:2:66,222] Security, and other federal agencies. Additionally, Cal OES serves as an advocate for local public safety, emergency  

[7:3:66,232] management, and public and private agencies operating public safety and victim service programs. As part of its grant  

[7:4:66,243] management role, Cal OES administers the California Disaster Assistance Act and provides eligible local agencies with  

[7:5:66,253] financial assistance to recover from disasters. Included in this program is the State Threat Assessment Center, which is the  

[7:6:66,263] centerpiece of the state's intelligence information sharing environment and serves as the Governor's designated primary fusion  

[7:7:66,273] center.  

[8:0:66,291] 0390 - ALFRED E. ALQUIST SEISMIC SAFETY COMMISSION  

[9:0:66,309] The Alfred E. Alquist Seismic Safety Commission program provides a coordinated framework for establishing consistent  

[9:1:66,319] earthquake policies; advising the Governor, the Legislature, local governments, and the public; and tracking the state’s  

[9:2:66,329] progress toward higher levels of seismic safety.   The Commission uses the expertise of its commissioners to review, evaluate,  

[9:3:66,339] and translate scientific information and make recommendations to guide and influence earthquake safety policies.   The  

[9:4:66,349] Commission works with federal, state, and local agencies, as well as the private sector, on a variety of activities that support  

[9:5:66,360] the state’s earthquake preparedness, mitigation, response, and recovery efforts.   These activities include: (1) developing and  

[9:6:66,370] reviewing seismic safety projects; (2) providing consistent policy direction; (3) proposing and reviewing earthquake-related  

[9:7:66,380] legislation; (4) conducting public hearings on seismic safety issues; (5) using existing knowledge and conducting studies,  

[9:8:66,390] where necessary, to improve the performance of structures in California, (6) recommending earthquake safety programs and  

[9:9:66,400] supporting cost-effective partnerships that help reduce earthquake risks and speed economic recovery.  

[10:0:66,418] 0395 - PUBLIC SAFETY COMMUNICATIONS  

[11:0:66,439] The Public Safety Communications   program is responsible for the design, installation, maintenance, and repair of the  

[11:1:66,449] state's   public safety communications networks and oversight of the state's 9-1-1 system. The   program is dedicated to the  

[11:2:66,459] preservation and protection of human life and public safety by delivering reliable and dependable communication services that  

[11:3:66,469] keep the public connected during times of crisis.  

[12:0:66,487] 9900 - ADMINISTRATION AND EXECUTIVE  

[13:0:66,507] The Administration and Executive Program provides the overall policy direction of   Cal OES and support services such as  

[13:1:66,517] accounting, fiscal, personnel, information technology, and business services.

[3:0:66,69] The Office of Emergency Services' infrastructure includes a headquarters facility, an administrative building, and an Inland  

[3:1:66,79] Regional Operations Center in Sacramento County; a Coastal Regional Operations Center in Fairfield in Solano County; a  

[3:2:66,89] Southern Regional Operations Center located at Los Alamitos Joint Forces Training Base in Orange County; the California  

[3:3:66,99] Specialized Training Institute at Camp San Luis Obispo; and various small field offices throughout the state.  

[3:4:66,117] The Public Safety Communications division (PSC) is comprised of a main leased complex in Sacramento and 48 field locations  

[3:5:66,127] throughout the state. These locations include 7 area offices and 41 area shops, positioned geographically to facilitate  

[3:6:66,137] maintenance and installation services to remote communication sites and customers throughout the state. In addition, the PSC  

[3:7:66,148] maintains and operates 11 communications vaults/towers and a total of more than 3,500 radio frequency points of presence.
