[2:0:116,32] 0974   California Pollution Control Financing Authority - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] 0860 - POLLUTION CONTROL TAX-EXEMPT BOND PROGRAM  

[4:0:66,86] CPCFA’s Tax-Exempt Bond Program provides access to the tax-exempt bond market for private companies serving a public  

[4:1:66,97] purpose, through the use of private activity bonds, for California businesses, for the acquisition, construction, or installation of  

[4:2:66,107] qualified waste disposal, waste recovery, water furnishing, and wastewater treatment, or the acquisition and installation of new  

[4:3:66,117] equipment. Small businesses may receive financial assistance towards the costs of issuance.  

[5:0:66,135] Tax-exempt financing issued by CPCFA assists municipalities in complying with waste diversion mandates of the California  

[5:1:66,145] Integrated Waste Management Act of 1989, the California Global Warming Solutions Act of 2006, and more recently, Assembly  

[5:2:66,155] Bill 1826 (Chesbro, Chapter 727, Statutes of 2014), and Senate Bill 1383 (Lara, Chapter 395, Statutes of 2016). As of June 30,  

[5:3:66,165] 2021, bonds totaling approximately $16.7 billion have been issued by CPCFA.  

[6:0:66,183] 0865 - CALIFORNIA CAPITAL ACCESS PROGRAM FOR SMALL BUSINESSES  

[7:0:66,201] CalCAP for Small Business incentivizes banks and other financial institutions to make loans to small businesses through the  

[7:1:66,211] use of a loan loss reserve account. The program works through   the creation of   a loan loss-reserve fund specific to  

[7:2:66,221] each   participating financial institution. CPCFA, the financial institution, and the borrower all contribute to fund the reserve.  

[7:3:66,231] CPCFA provides additional   incentives for   lending to businesses located in underserved communities and disaster areas. The  

[7:4:66,241] reserve serves to reduce loan-loss risk, supporting financial institutions when they lend to California small businesses.  

[8:0:66,259] Prior to 2010, CalCAP was entirely self-funded with $38 million transferred from CPCFA’s Small Business Assistance Fund  

[8:1:66,269] (SBAF), established   with fees collected from large companies utilizing CPCFA's   tax-exempt bond program.   In October 2010  

[8:2:66,280] CalCAP received $6 million from the General Fund (Chapter 731, Statutes of 2010) to increase lending efforts.   The $6 million  

[8:3:66,290] appropriation   was fully-expended by the end of 2018 allowing the annual recapture of contributions to become the primary  

[8:4:66,300] source for the State's contributions.  

[9:0:66,320] In   2011 California was awarded an allocation of federal funds in the amount of $168 million from U.S. Treasury's State Small  

[9:1:66,331] Business Credit Initiative (SSBCI). CPCFA shared the allocation with the Infrastructure and Economic Development Bank  

[9:2:66,341] (IBank), now housed within the Governor's Office of Business and Economic Development (GO-Biz). 

[10:0:66,358] These funds enabled CPCFA to expand the availability of its CalCAP for Small Business (CalCAP SB).   In addition, CPCFA  

[10:1:66,369] launched the   Collateral Support Program (CSP)   to   further offset financial institution’s resistance to lend after the 2008  

[10:2:66,379] recession. CPCFA created the CSP with a recapture provision allowing for the sustainability of the program.  

[11:0:66,397] The SSBCI program ended in 2017. In an effort to sustain the program past the sunset date, CalCAP SB instituted a recapture  

[11:1:66,407] provision. Funds are being   recaptured annually to sustain the CalCAP SB and the CSP.  

[12:0:66,424] In 2021, the federal government reauthorized the funding of SSBCI (SSBCI 2.0).   CPCFA and IBank partnered again to apply  

[12:1:66,435] for SSBCI 2.0 allocation and will share the $1.181 billion allocated to California.  

[13:0:66,452] Utilizing the CalCAP model, CPCFA partnered with the California Air Resources Board (CARB) to assist   truck and bus owners  

[13:1:66,463] in meeting new clean air requirements by directing a total of $215.9 million in Air Quality Improvement Program funds to  

[13:2:66,473] incentivize lending for cleaner vehicles that   comply with CARB's Statewide Truck and Bus Rule. CPCFA also partnered with the  

[13:3:66,483] California Energy Commission (CEC) to implement a $2 million pilot program to provide credit enhancements and rebates to  

[13:4:66,493] small business borrowers and multi-unit dwellings to   install electric vehicle charging stations. The CalCAP Electric Vehicle  

[13:5:66,503] Charging Station (EVCS) Financing Program ended March 31, 2022.  

[14:0:66,521] 0870 - CALIFORNIA RECYCLE UNDERUTILIZED SITES PROGRAM  

[15:0:66,541] The California Recycle Underutilized Sites (CALReUSE) program assists with the reuse and redevelopment of underutilized  

[15:1:66,552] properties with real or perceived contamination issues. CALReUSE addresses a funding and information gap in the  

[15:2:66,562] development of contaminated properties (brownfields) to help bring these properties into productive reuse. Beginning in 2000,  

[15:3:66,572] the CALReUSE Assessment Program provided forgivable loans of up to $300,000 which was later increased to $500,000 per  

[15:4:66,582] brownfield site. The funds assisted with brownfield site assessment and characterization, technical assistance, and the  

[15:5:66,592] development of remedial action plans. Eligible projects included sites with potential beneficial reuse not currently redeveloped  

[15:6:66,603] due to lack of information about real or perceived contamination, uncertainty about clean-up costs, or concerns regarding time  

[15:7:66,613] frames and the regulatory process.  

[16:0:66,633] Additionally, Proposition 1C, the Housing and Emergency Shelter Trust Fund Act of 2006, included provisions to fund  

[16:1:66,643] brownfield cleanup that resulted in the expansion of the CALReUSE Program. The CALReUSE Remediation Program received  

[16:2:66,654] $60 million in Proposition 1C funds and provided loans and grants up to $5 million per project   for   brownfield remediation that  

[16:3:66,664] promoted affordable infill residential and mixed-used developments. Three projects were pre-approved to receive awards of  

[16:4:66,674] more than $5 million; one grant was increased above $5 million when returned funds became available. All CALReUSE  

[16:5:66,684] Program funds have been awarded. The CALReUSE Program's $60 million investment in cleanup of contaminated sites will  

[16:6:66,694] result in 5,587 housing units, of which more than 47% will be affordable, in 30 California communities.  

[17:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] 0875 - ALTERNATIVE FINANCING PROGRAMS  

[4:0:66,89] Under its authority to provide alternative methods of financing facilities for pollution control, clean water supply and alternative  

[4:1:66,99] energy production, CPCFA is developing alternative financing models to assist sister agencies with achieving the state's  

[4:2:66,109] environmental goals. CPCFA is authorized to receive and accept contributions from other sources including federal and state  

[4:3:66,120] agencies to carry out the purposes of its statutory authority.  

[4:4:66,137] 0876 - CALIFORNIA CAPITAL ACCESS PROGRAM FOR AMERICANS WITH DISABILITIES  

[4:5:66,155] The objective of the California Capital Access program for Americans with Disabilities (CalCAP/ADA) created by Chapter 787,  

[4:6:66,165] Statutes of 2015, is to help small businesses comply with the federal Americans with Disabilities Act (ADA) and increase  

[4:7:66,175] access for   employees and customers by encouraging lenders and other financial institutions to make loans to very small  

[4:8:66,186] businesses to finance tenant improvements. The Legislature provided CPCFA an appropriation of $10 million effective January  

[4:9:66,196] 1, 2016, to administer a credit enhancement program following the CalCAP model   incentivizing lenders to enroll qualifying  

[4:10:66,206] loans.   The program also offers borrowers a rebate toward the cost of the Certified Access Specialist (CAS) Report.  

[4:11:66,224] 0877 - CALIFORNIA SEISMIC SAFETY CAPITAL ACCESS LOAN PROGRAM  

[5:0:66,244] The California Seismic Safety Capital Access Loan Program, authorized by Chapter 32, Statutes of 2016, provided a one-time  

[5:1:66,254] allocation of $10 million effective January 1, 2017 to administer a credit enhancement   program for   qualified small businesses  

[5:2:66,265] and residential property owners (including multi-unit dwellings and mobile homes registered by the California Department of  

[5:3:66,275] Housing and Community Development) for costs related to seismic retrofitting. The Program encourages banks and other  

[5:4:66,285] financial institutions to participate by creating loan loss reserve accounts for lenders that enroll qualifying loans into the  

[5:5:66,295] program.  

[6:0:66,313] 0878 - CALIFORNIA INVESTMENT AND INNOVATION PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,331] The California Investment and Innovation Program is authorized by Chapter 68, Statutes of 2022, (SB 193) and was provided a  

[7:1:66,341] one-time allocation of $50 million from the General Fund into a new special fund, the California Investment and Innovation  

[7:2:66,351] Fund, to support a new grant program to provide local assistance to the state’s Community Development Financial Institutions  

[7:3:66,361] (CDFIs).

[9:0:154,404] 0977   California Health Facilities Financing Authority  

[10:0:66,426] The mission of the California Health Facilities Financing Authority is to help eligible and credit worthy nonprofit and public  

[10:1:66,436] health facilities reduce their cost of capital, and promote health care improvement and cost containment objectives by providing  

[10:2:66,446] cost-effective tax-exempt bond, low-cost loan, and direct grant programs.  

[11:0:66,464] The Authority was established by Chapter 1033, Statutes of 1979 (AB 1558), and consists of nine members: the State  

[11:1:66,474] Treasurer, the Director of the Department of Finance, the State Controller, two public members appointed by the Senate, two  

[11:2:66,484] public members appointed by the Assembly, and two public members appointed by the Governor.

[9:0:66,539] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[9:1:66,558] 0880 - CHILDREN'S HOSPITAL PROGRAM  

[10:0:66,576] In 2004, California voters approved Proposition 61, which established the Children's Hospital Program. In 2008, Proposition 3  

[10:1:66,586] established the second Children's Hospital Program. In 2018, Proposition 4 established a third Children’s Hospital Program.  

[10:2:66,596] The purpose of the three programs is to improve the health and welfare of California's critically ill children by providing a stable  

[10:3:66,606] and ready source of funds for capital improvement projects for children's hospitals and, under Proposition 4, hospitals that  

[10:4:66,616] provide pediatric services for children eligible for California Children’s Services. Children's Hospital Program grant funding is  

[10:5:66,627] derived from the issuance of general obligation bonds, up to $750 million under Proposition 61, $980 million under Proposition  

[10:6:66,637] 3, and $1.5 billion under Proposition 4.  

[10:7:66,655] 0885 - HEALTH FACILITIES BONDS, LOANS, AND GRANTS  

[11:0:66,675] The Authority serves as a conduit provider of tax-exempt bonds and a direct provider of financial assistance to health  

[11:1:66,685] institutions.  

[12:0:66,706] Tax-Exempt Bond Program  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 256 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] 2022-23 STATE BUDGET — LJE   254 

[1:0:382,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:119,32] 0977   California Health Facilities Financing Authority - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] Bonds issued under this conduit program are not a debt, liability, or a pledge of the full faith and credit of the taxing power of  

[3:1:66,79] the state or any of its political subdivisions. The full faith and credit of the participating institutions are pledged for repayment of  

[3:2:66,89] the bonds. To qualify for funding, the proposed project must be a health facility, operated by a private nonprofit corporation or  

[3:3:66,99] association, city, city and county, county, or hospital district.  

[4:0:66,120] HELP II Loan Program  

[5:0:66,137] The Authority administers the Healthcare Expansion Loan Program II (HELP II) to assist small and rural health facilities in  

[5:1:66,148] obtaining financing for their capital needs. The HELP II Program provides two percent and three percent fixed interest rate  

[5:2:66,158] loans of up to $2 million to California's nonprofit small and rural health facilities in an efficient, timely, and cost-effective manner.  

[5:3:66,168] HELP II loans may be used to purchase or construct new facilities, remodel or renovate existing facilities, purchase equipment  

[5:4:66,178] and/or furnishings, and refinance existing debt.  

[6:0:66,196] 0890 - MENTAL HEALTH WELLNESS GRANTS  

[7:0:66,214] • Investment in Mental Health Wellness Act of 2013 Grant Program 

[8:0:66,231] Chapter 34, Statutes of 2013 (SB 82), the Investment in Mental Health Wellness Act of 2013 and related legislation provided  

[8:1:66,241] $142.5 million in one-time General Fund, and $4 million in ongoing Mental Health Services Act funding to provide grants for  

[8:2:66,252] community based mental health crisis support.  

[9:0:66,269] The Act established a grant program to disburse funds to California counties or to their nonprofit or public agency designees for  

[9:1:66,280] the purpose of developing mental health crisis support programs. Specifically, funds may be used to increase capacity for client  

[9:2:66,290] assistance and services in crisis intervention, crisis stabilization, crisis residential treatment, rehabilitative mental health  

[9:3:66,300] services, and mobile crisis support teams. The grants will support capital improvement, expansion and limited start-up costs.  

[10:0:66,320] • Investment in Mental Health Wellness Grant Program for Children and Youth  

[11:0:66,338] Chapter 30, Statutes of 2016 (SB 833), Section 20 created the Investment in Mental Health Wellness Grant Program for  

[11:1:66,348] Children and Youth to increase mental health services for children and youth 21 years of age and under to develop a complete  

[11:2:66,358] continuum of crisis services. Working with counties, the program funds facility acquisition, construction/renovation, equipment  

[11:3:66,369] acquisition, applicable startup or expansion costs, and limited personnel funding to develop crisis residential treatment and  

[11:4:66,379] crisis stabilization facilities, mobile crisis support teams, and family respite care facilities.  

[12:0:66,399] • Community Services Infrastructure Grants 

[13:0:66,417] Chapter 33, Statutes of 2016 (SB 843), Section 52 created the Community Services Infrastructure Grant Program to expand  

[13:1:66,427] community alternatives to jail and prison. The program seeks to expand access to jail and prison diversion programs and  

[13:2:66,437] services for those with mental health illness, substance use disorders, or who have suffered from trauma. Working with  

[13:3:66,448] counties, the program funds facility acquisition, renovation, equipment acquisition, and applicable startup or expansion costs for  

[13:4:66,458] facilities that provide services to this population.  

[14:0:66,475] • No Place Like Home Program  

[15:0:66,493] Chapter 43, Statutes of 2016 (AB 1618) established the No Place Like Home (NPLH) Program, which requires the Department  

[15:1:66,503] of Housing and Community Development (HCD) to award up to $2 billion to counties for the acquisition, design, construction,  

[15:2:66,514] rehabilitation, or preservation of permanent supportive housing for individuals living with a severe mental illness who are  

[15:3:66,524] homeless or at risk of chronic homelessness. Chapter 322, Statutes of 2016 (AB 1628) authorized CHFFA to enter into  

[15:4:66,534] contracts with HCD and further authorized CHFFA to issue up to $2 billion in taxable revenue bonds pursuant to the NPLH  

[15:5:66,544] Program. Chapter 41, Statutes of 2018 (AB 1827) placed the NPLH Program on the November 2018 ballot (Proposition 2),  

[15:6:66,554] where it was adopted by the voters as the No Place Like Home Act. This ratified existing law establishing the NPLH Program  

[15:7:66,565] as being consistent with the Mental Health Services Act approved through Proposition 63 in 2004. It also ratified the issuance  

[15:8:66,575] of up to $2 billion in previously authorized bonds.

[10:0:66,474] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[10:1:66,493] 0895 - CALIFORNIA ABLE ACT BOARD  

[11:0:66,514] On December 19, 2014, President Obama signed the Stephen Beck Jr., Achieving a Better Life Experience Act of 2014, which  

[11:1:66,524] allows individuals who became blind or disabled before reaching age 26, to create tax-free savings accounts. Individuals can  

[11:2:66,534] make nondeductible cash contributions to an ABLE account in the name of a designated beneficiary with tax-free earnings.  

[11:3:66,544] ABLE account distributions are also not included in the beneficiary's income, as long as they are used for qualified disability  

[11:4:66,555] expenses. Chapter 774, Statutes of 2015 (AB 449), implements the ABLE Act in California, and directs the Board, with the  

[11:5:66,565] State Treasurer as Chair, to administer the California ABLE Program accounts on behalf of eligible individuals.  

[12:0:66,585] The Board and the California ABLE Program Trust were established by Chapter 796, Statutes of 2015 (SB 324), for the  

[12:1:66,595] purpose of creating the Program. CalABLE was officially launched in December of 2018 with the mission to meet the diverse  

[12:2:66,606] needs of our customers and their families. CalABLE’s pledge is to be customer-driven, accountable, and a trusted partner in  

[12:3:66,616] providing financial services.  

[13:0:66,633] Under the Program, a person may make contributions of up to $15,000 during a taxable year, to a designated beneficiary's  

[13:1:66,644] ABLE account to be used for the qualified disability expenses of that designated beneficiary.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[5:1:66,148] Statutes of 2016 (SB 1234), subsequently authorized the Board to implement the CalSavers Retirement Savings Program  

[5:2:66,158] (CalSavers), a state-administered retirement savings program for private sector employees in California with no access to  

[5:3:66,168] workplace retirement savings plans. The Board’s mission is to ensure all Californians have a path to financial security in  

[5:4:66,178] retirement by providing a simple, portable, low-cost way for workers to invest in their futures.  

[6:0:66,196] The Board consists of nine members: the State Treasurer (Chairperson), the Director of the Department of Finance, the State  

[6:1:66,206] Controller, a retirement savings and investment expert appointed by the Senate Committee on Rules, an employee  

[6:2:66,217] representative appointed by the Speaker of the Assembly, a small business representative appointed by the Governor, a public

[5:0:66,180] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,199] 0910 - CALSAVERS  

[6:0:66,217] The Program provides workers access to a simple, portable, low-cost retirement savings account. Any private sector employer  

[6:1:66,227] with at least five employees that does not offer a workplace retirement savings vehicle by the statutory compliance deadline is  

[6:2:66,237] required to provide their employees with access to the Program. Most employers are required to register by June 30, 2022, and  

[6:3:66,247] newly-eligible employers are required to register by the end of each calendar year. CalSavers launched statewide on July 1,

[9:0:185,439] 0985   California School Finance Authority  

[10:0:66,461] The California School Finance Authority (CSFA), created in 1985, provides facilities and working capital financing to school  

[10:1:66,471] districts, community college districts, county offices of education, and charter schools. The State Treasurer serves as chair of  

[10:2:66,481] CSFA, and the Superintendent of Public Instruction and the Director of Finance serve as members. CSFA has offices in Los  

[10:3:66,491] Angeles and Sacramento.  

[11:0:66,509] CSFA serves as the primary state agency for California charter schools seeking facility and working capital assistance by  

[11:1:66,519] providing access to low-cost financing through several state and federally funded programs.

[5:0:66,154] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,173] 0918 - SMART BONDS PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,194] Chapter 1438, Statutes of 1985, as amended by Chapter 598, Statutes of 1987, established CSFA and authorized the issuance  

[7:1:66,204] of revenue bonds or other debt instruments. The proceeds from the sale of the bonds are available for loans to schools and  

[7:2:66,214] community college districts to assist with the acquisition of equipment and new school sites, construction of new facilities,  

[7:3:66,224] reconstruction of existing facilities, capital improvements, and to provide working capital. Chapter 325, Statutes of 2006 (AB  

[7:4:66,234] 2717), allows CSFA to issue debt on behalf of California charter schools for capital improvements and working capital needs.  

[8:0:66,252] 0920 - CHARTER SCHOOL FACILITIES PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,273] Chapter 935, Statutes of 2002, as amended by Chapter 587, Statutes of 2003, created the Charter School Facilities Program  

[9:1:66,283] (CSFP), which provides funding for the new construction, renovation or rehabilitation of charter school facilities. CSFA and the  

[9:2:66,293] Office of Public School Construction jointly administer the program. CSFP was funded through the Kindergarten-University  

[9:3:66,303] Public Education Facilities Bond Acts of 2002, 2004, 2006, and 2016, for a total of $1.4 billion. The program provides a 50  

[9:4:66,313] percent state subsidy for charter school facilities project costs, with the balance of the project costs being repaid (to the state)  

[9:5:66,324] by the charter school via a 30-year loan. Recipient charter schools must provide site-based instruction, be financially sound,  

[9:6:66,334] and meet other program eligibility requirements. 

[10:0:66,351] 0925 - STATE CHARTER SCHOOL FACILITIES INCENTIVE GRANTS PROGRAM  

[11:0:66,369] In 2004, 2009, and 2014, CSFA received grants under the United States Department of Education’s State Charter School  

[11:1:66,379] Facilities Incentive Grants Program (CFDA 84.282D) to provide California charter schools with assistance for facilities costs.  

[11:2:66,390] Grant funds reimburse a charter school’s costs for rent, lease, mortgage or debt service payments for existing or new facilities  

[11:3:66,400] or the costs of acquiring land and/or constructing or renovating a facility. Grants are awarded to charter schools based on  

[11:4:66,410] preference points assigned based on: the percentage of low-income students; percentage of overcrowding; not-for-profit status;  

[11:5:66,420] and demonstrated student performance. Schools cannot use program funds to supplant state funding. This program is  

[11:6:66,430] sunsetting in August 2022.  

[12:0:66,448] 0930 - CHARTER SCHOOL FACILITY GRANT PROGRAM  

[13:0:66,466] The Charter School Facility Grant Program provides Proposition 98 General Fund grants to assist charter schools with rent and  

[13:1:66,476] lease costs. This program targets schools and communities with significant populations of economically disadvantaged  

[13:2:66,486] students. Eligible charter schools are provided the lesser of $1,313 per unit of eligible average daily attendance, or 75 percent  

[13:3:66,496] of annual facilities rent and lease costs.  

[14:0:66,514] 0935 - CHARTER SCHOOL REVOLVING LOAN FUND  

[15:0:66,532] Charter 1010, Statutes of 1976 established the Charter School Revolving Loan Fund in the State Treasury to provide loans of  

[15:1:66,542] up to $250,000 to new charter schools to assist them in meeting the purposes of the school's approved charter. The program is  

[15:2:66,552] available to any charter school that is not a conversion of an existing public school, and preference goes to schools that have  

[15:3:66,562] not opened nor completed their full charter term.

[12:0:168,661] 0989   California Educational Facilities Authority  

[12:1:66,682] The California Educational Facilities Authority (CEFA) provides assistance to qualified nonprofit higher education institutions  

[12:2:66,692] through a tax-exempt revenue bond program to reduce the costs of financing academic facilities.

[11:0:66,621] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[11:1:66,640] 0940 - BOND FINANCING  

[12:0:66,658] CEFA issues tax-exempt revenue bonds to assist private educational institutions of higher learning to construct educational  

[12:1:66,668] facilities. Because it is authorized to issue tax-exempt bonds, CEFA may be able to provide more favorable financing terms  

[12:2:66,678] than might otherwise be obtainable. CEFA also may include qualifying nonprofit entities as eligible program participants for the  

[12:3:66,689] construction of student and faculty housing. Bonds issued by CEFA are not a debt, liability, or claim on the faith and credit or  

[12:4:66,699] the taxing power of the State of California or any of its political subdivisions. The full faith and credit of the participating  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 273 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:410,13] 2022-23 STATE BUDGET — LJE   271 

[2:0:133,32] 0989   California Educational Facilities Authority - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] institution is pledged to the payment of the bonds. Bonds issued for this purpose are not subject to the state's "private activity"  

[3:1:66,79] bond ceiling as specified in federal law.  

[4:0:66,109] For purposes of the California Educational Facilities Act, "private college" or "participating private college" means a nonprofit,  

[4:1:66,119] private, regionally accredited, degree-granting college that does not restrict the admission of a student based on the student's  

[4:2:66,129] race or ethnicity and provided that the financing does not violate constitutional provisions.  

[5:0:66,159] "Bond" means bonds, notes, debentures, securities, or other evidences of indebtedness. This broad definition allows CEFA to  

[5:1:66,169] issue tax-exempt public offering or private placement loans for participating institutions, which may provide greater cost savings  

[5:2:66,180] than these colleges and universities might otherwise realize on a traditional bond financing.  

[6:0:66,199] 0955 - COLLEGE ACCESS TAX CREDIT FUND  

[7:0:66,217] Chapter 367, Statutes of 2014, required CEFA to administer the College Access Tax Credit Fund and allocate and certify the  

[7:1:66,227] tax credits for taxable years beginning on or after January 1, 2014, and before January 1, 2017. Chapter 22, Statutes of 2015,  

[7:2:66,238] added a requirement that CEFA continue to allocate and certify the tax credits for taxable years beginning on or after January  

[7:3:66,248] 1, 2017, and before January 1, 2018. Chapter 527, Statutes of 2017, extended CEFA's responsibility to administer the tax  

[7:4:66,258] credits for taxable years beginning on or after January 1, 2017, and before January 1, 2023.
